54:1725:114259:18:18:71:539:331:145:g_d0_f6:145:g_d0_f2:B:log19902−log19902etc.  Finally, to account for random sampling error in
54:1726:114357:18:18:71:539:313:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:both the distribution of idiosyncratic shocks and the random path of past aggregate shocks,
54:1727:114448:18:0:71:417:295:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:we repeat this 250 times and average the results across simulations.
55:1728:114518:0:35:72:471:708:301:g_d0_f1:301:g_d0_f1:C:10  Appendix 3: Estimation and Identification
55:1729:114563:35:25:72:538:673:209:g_d0_f1:209:g_d0_f1:F:10.1  Estimation Algorithm and Construction of Standard Errors
55:1730:114625:25:18:72:540:648:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:In this section we provide additional details on our estimation algorithm, the particular
55:1731:114714:18:18:72:539:630:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:functional form we choose forand the construction of standard errors for point estimates
55:1732:114804:18:18:71:167:612:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:and model targets.
55:1733:114822:18:18:89:539:594:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:A key requirement for our estimation algorithm is the construction of a function and set
55:1734:114910:18:18:71:540:576:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:of observables that solves:=()First, note that as long as−1∈then predicting
55:1735:114993:18:18:71:544:558:145:g_d0_f5:145:g_d0_f2:B:is equivalent to predicting∗since=log∗−log(−1)Sincewewillassumethat
55:1736:115067:18:18:71:539:540:145:g_d0_f5:145:g_d0_f2:B:−1is in the econometrician’s information set, we thus change notation and search for a
55:1737:115154:18:18:71:538:522:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f5:B:function∗=()The most straightforward way to construct()is to make
55:1738:115228:18:18:71:540:504:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:equal to the agents’ (empirically observable) state-variables and thenwill be equal to
55:1739:115316:18:17:71:539:486:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:the policy function that solves∗(−1−1)=argmax(−1−1)While this
55:1740:115396:17:18:71:539:469:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:function would exactly map observable states to choices in a world with perfect data, it is
55:1741:115487:18:18:71:539:451:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:problematic in a world with measurement error.  Since this function is non-linear, even if
55:1742:115577:18:18:71:539:433:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:measurement error is on average zero it need not produce estimates which are on average
55:1743:115664:18:18:71:539:415:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:correct.  That is,[∗(−1−1)]6=∗(−1−1): if state-variables are measured
55:1744:115749:18:18:71:539:397:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:with noise and used as inputs to a highly non-linear function, then the resulting estimate
55:1745:115839:18:18:71:539:379:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:for∗need not be an unbiased estimate of the truth.   Given this concern, we instead
55:1746:115923:18:18:72:414:361:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:approximateby a linear function of various observable variables.
55:1747:115988:18:18:89:539:343:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:In particular, we assume that()=0+1−1+2−1+3+4−1In practice,
55:1748:116061:18:18:71:539:325:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:this functional form is highly accurate along various different metrics. First, we can ask how
55:1749:116155:18:18:71:540:307:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:well this linear function does at predicting actual model∗when there is no measurement
55:1750:116242:18:18:72:539:289:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:error.  That is, given the true values forhow well do we predict the actual∗in the
55:1751:116327:18:18:71:540:271:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:model? Running our regression of∗ondelivers an2=098. Thus, we do not perfectly
55:1752:116409:18:18:72:540:253:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:match model gaps in an environment with no measurement error, but this function does an
55:1753:116496:18:17:72:540:235:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:extremely good job. (Note that using the true policy function would by construction deliver
55:1754:116587:17:18:72:540:218:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:an2=1).  We can next ask how well we do at matching the true∗implied byif we

55:1757:116836:18:18:71:540:164:145:g_d0_f5:145:g_d0_f2:B:2=85, so even with noisily measured inputs, we are able to well predict actual durable
55:1758:116924:18:18:71:539:146:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:gaps in the model. In addition wefind thatb∗=∗In contrast, if we apply the model’s
55:1759:117010:18:18:71:540:128:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:true policy function to noisily measured state-variables then wefind an2=060and we

55:1761:117183:18:0:72:539:92:145:g_d0_f2:65:g_d0_f12:B:measurement error and outputs does a more successful job of producing the true model∗
56:1762:117271:0:18:72:488:708:145:g_d0_f2:65:g_d0_f2:B:then does imposing actual model policy functions on these mismeasured inputs.63
56:1763:117350:18:18:89:539:690:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:Thus, thethat we choose performs quite well.  Nevertheless, we have explored vari-
56:1764:117434:18:18:71:539:672:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:ous alternative functional forms including adding−1−1+−1,,andas additional predictors.
56:1765:117526:18:18:72:540:654:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:However, none of these alternatives provide much additional predictive power in the envi-
56:1766:117615:18:18:72:540:636:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:ronment with no measurement error and they perform less well in an environments where
56:1767:117700:18:12:72:540:618:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:variables are measured with noise. Since many households hold no liquid assets, introducing
56:1768:117791:12:10:83:97:606:65:g_d0_f7:36:g_d0_f22:A:−1
56:1769:117794:10:14:73:549:596:65:g_d0_f7:145:g_d0_f2:B:−1+−1as a predictor introduces collinearity issues when identifying the effect of−1.We
56:1770:117883:14:18:72:540:582:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:use information on consumption rather than earnings in our baseline specification because
56:1771:117972:18:18:72:540:564:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:earnings is more frequently missing as households have spells of unemployment. Simulations
56:1772:118062:18:18:72:539:546:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:in the model suggest that we lose essentially no accuracy by usinginstead ofwhen there
56:1773:118150:18:18:72:540:528:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:is no measurement error, and we gain substantial additional predictive power when there
56:1774:118237:18:18:72:540:510:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:is random missing data onUsing ratios ofis again problematic for households with
56:1775:118321:18:17:71:539:492:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:no earnings.  Nevertheless, while these alternative functions seem to do slightly less well in
56:1776:118415:17:18:71:540:475:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:simulation at predicting true gaps in the model, we have re-run our benchmark model using

57:1861:120161:18:18:89:539:647:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:Steps 1-7 describe the procedure for constructing point estimates.  To construct boot-
57:1862:120247:18:18:72:305:629:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:strapped standard errors we do the following:
57:1863:120292:18:18:89:476:611:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:8) Given our parameter point estimates, simulate data as in step 2) above.
57:1864:120366:18:18:89:539:593:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:9)  Replace the PSID data in the previous estimation with the "fake data" simulated
57:1865:120449:18:18:72:276:575:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:from the model bestfit point estimates.
57:1866:120488:18:18:89:540:557:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:10) Re-estimate the model tofind point estimates which bestfit the new "fake" PSID
57:1867:120570:18:18:71:97:539:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:data.
57:1868:120575:18:17:89:539:521:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:11)  Record point estimates and implied hazards for this bootstrap replication.  Also
57:1869:120660:17:18:71:539:504:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:record the implied distribution of gaps and hazards in the actual PSID data under this new
57:1870:120750:18:18:71:148:486:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:point estimate.
57:1871:120765:18:18:89:539:468:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:12) Repeat 8-12 1000 times to construct a distribution of bootstrapped standard errors
57:1872:120851:18:39:71:240:450:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:that accounts for sampling error.
57:1873:120884:39:26:72:210:411:209:g_d0_f1:209:g_d0_f1:C:10.2  Identification
57:1874:120904:26:18:72:539:385:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:How are parameters identified in our model?  As usual in numerical models, we have no
57:1875:120989:18:18:72:539:367:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:proofofglobalidentification, but in practice we parameterize our hazard and density using
57:1876:121078:18:18:71:540:349:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:21 bins for each. This means that we have 42 targeted moments and onlyfive parameters so
57:1877:121166:18:18:71:540:331:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:thatthemodelappearstobeoveridentified. In addition, starting the search for the bestfit
57:1878:121253:18:17:71:540:313:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:parameters at various starting values converges to the same best-fit results, which suggests
57:1879:121345:17:18:71:258:296:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:that the model is globally identified.
57:1880:121383:18:18:89:539:278:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:Furthermore, we can argue more strongly for local identification by varying individual
57:1881:121469:18:18:71:539:260:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:parameters holding othersfixed at their bestfit estimates.  Each of our parameters induces
57:1882:121559:18:18:71:539:242:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:independent variation on the model and data densities and hazards. In the followingfigures,
57:1883:121650:18:18:71:539:224:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:we change one parameter at a time and explore its implications for model and data densities
57:1884:121741:18:18:71:539:206:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:and hazards.  In eachfigure, the blue lines correspond to model objects and green lines to
57:1885:121831:18:0:71:142:188:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:PSID objects.

59:2029:122735:0:0:322:326:151:58:Helvetica:58:Helvetica:?:1
60:2030:122738:0:336:166:444:709:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:C:Figure 24: Changing Proportional Measurement Error
60:2031:122788:336:18:89:540:373:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:Changes in thefirst four parameters induce changes in both model and empirical densities.
60:2032:122877:18:18:71:540:355:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:This is because as we change these parameters we alter the functionthat maps observ-
60:2033:122963:18:0:72:507:337:145:g_d0_f2:65:g_d0_f7:B:ables to gaps in the model.  This in turn induces variation in PSID gaps:b=
60:2034:123042:0:9:509:516:350:145:g_d0_f9:145:g_d0_f9:C:³
60:2035:123043:9:0:518:526:341:145:g_d0_f9:65:g_d0_f7:?:b
60:2036:123046:0:13:527:534:350:145:g_d0_f9:145:g_d0_f9:C: ́
60:2037:123048:13:20:536:539:337:145:g_d0_f5:145:g_d0_f5:C:
60:2038:123049:20:18:72:540:317:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:In contrast, introducing measurement error affectsc=(1+b)butitdoesnotaffect the
60:2039:123133:18:18:72:540:299:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:mapping between true model observables (with no measurement error) and outcomes. That
60:2040:123218:18:18:72:540:281:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:is, measurement error does not affectAs such, changing the degree of measurement
60:2041:123301:18:18:72:539:263:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:error has no effect on the gaps and hazard imputed in PSID and only affects the gaps and
60:2042:123389:18:17:72:540:245:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:hazards imputed for model simulated data (with measurement error).  Since changing the
60:2043:123475:17:18:72:540:228:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:measurement error parameter has no effectonPSIDweplotitsaffect only on model densities
60:2044:123561:18:18:72:540:210:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:and hazards.  In addition, we show how the loss function varies as we change measurement
60:2045:123649:18:18:72:338:192:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:error to demonstrate that there is a clear minimum.
60:2046:123700:18:18:89:539:174:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:However, it is worth noting that the variation in densities and hazards induced by mea-
60:2047:123787:18:18:72:540:156:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:surement error is substantially less than that induced by the other parameters of our model,
60:2048:123879:18:18:72:540:138:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:so if any parameter is not particularly well-identified it is probably the amount of measure-
60:2049:123972:18:18:72:540:120:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:ment error.  However, this is not a huge concern for our results as we are not particularly
60:2050:124063:18:18:72:540:102:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:interested in assessing the amount of measurement error in PSID variables.  Furthermore,
60:2051:124151:18:0:72:540:84:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:measurement error does not really affect any of our conclusions aside from their affect on
61:2052:124243:0:18:72:539:708:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:the overallfit and thus on the other parameters of their model.  True impulse responses
61:2053:124330:18:18:71:540:690:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:in the model are calculated without measurement error, so these IRFs are not affected by
61:2054:124418:18:18:71:539:672:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:changes in measurement error.  Since the measurement error parameter does not affect the
61:2055:124506:18:18:71:540:654:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:actual PSID data, changing its importance has no effect on the conclusions for the implied
61:2056:124596:18:18:71:261:636:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:IRF given by the PSID cross-section.
61:2057:124632:18:18:89:539:618:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:Finally, we can also construct surface plots for the loss-function as two different para-
61:2058:124721:18:18:71:540:600:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:meters are varied simultaneously.  For brevity we do not report these plots, but again the
61:2059:124811:18:39:71:274:582:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:model appears to be globally identified.
61:2060:124851:39:26:72:437:543:209:g_d0_f1:209:g_d0_f1:C:10.3  Applications to Alternative Models and Data
61:2061:124900:26:18:72:540:517:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:In this section, we explore two additional aspects of our estimation. 1) Can our estimation
61:2062:124991:18:17:72:539:499:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:procedure identify misspecified models?  2)  Does our model deliver useful out-of-sample
61:2063:125079:17:35:71:133:482:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:predictions?
61:2064:125091:35:46:71:329:447:145:g_d0_f1:145:g_d0_f1:C:10.3.1  Grossman and Laroque 1990 Model
61:2065:125130:46:292:136:474:401:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:C:Figure 25: Empirical Results for Grossman Laroque (1990) Model
61:2066:125192:292:18:89:540:109:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:First, to what extent is the empirical hazard actually a test for misspecification in our
61:2067:125281:18:18:71:540:91:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:model?  Since we target the density and hazarddirectly, perhaps it is just mechanical that
61:2068:125371:18:0:71:539:73:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:wefind a goodfit for these variables. To assess this, we apply our gap imputation procedure
62:2069:125464:0:18:72:540:708:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:to PSID data using the model from Grossman and Laroque [1990]. In this model, households
62:2070:125552:18:18:72:540:690:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:target a constant fraction of liquid wealth when adjusting, so it is straightforward to impute
62:2071:125646:18:18:72:540:672:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:durable gaps.  To what extent do these durable gaps provide predictive power for actual
62:2072:125733:18:18:72:540:654:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:adjustment patterns?  Figure 25 shows that the answer is: not at all.  Gaps imputed using
62:2073:125822:18:18:72:540:636:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:the structural model of 25 generate a nearlyflat hazard (varying from 0.06 to 0.12) but more
62:2074:125914:18:18:72:540:618:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:importantly, the hazard is not upward sloping in the absolute gap.  Households which are
62:2075:126002:18:18:72:410:600:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:predicted to adjust by the model are actually less likely to adjust.
62:2076:126070:18:18:89:539:582:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:Clearly the model of 25 is highly stylized since it ignores liquidity constraints and has
62:2077:126159:18:18:72:540:564:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:no non-durable consumption, and clearly these things matter empirically. The point of this
62:2078:126249:18:18:72:540:546:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:section is not that 25 is a bad model but is instead to illustrate that using an (S,s) model
62:2079:126341:18:18:72:539:528:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:with atheoreticalupward sloping hazard to impute gaps does not imply that the resulting
62:2080:126428:18:18:72:540:510:145:g_d0_f25:145:g_d0_f2:B:empiricalhazard need be upward sloping. In this sense, the behavior of the empirical hazard
62:2081:126519:18:18:72:540:492:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:implied by the structural model is indeed a good test for misspecification of the structural
62:2082:126611:18:34:71:105:474:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:model.
62:2083:126617:34:25:71:189:440:145:g_d0_f1:145:g_d0_f1:C:10.3.2  SHIW Data
62:2084:126634:25:18:71:539:415:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:Our model was estimated to match PSID data, and we showed that we canfind parameters
62:2085:126718:18:18:71:540:397:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:so that the model is a goodfit to the behavior of households in this sample.  In this sense,
62:2086:126810:18:18:71:540:379:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:our extremely strongfit is constructed "in-sample".  If we apply our same model estimates
62:2087:126899:18:18:71:539:361:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:to data on which our model is not directly estimated, do we continue tofind strong predictive
62:2088:126992:18:18:71:356:343:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:power?  In this section we argue that the answer is yes.
62:2089:127048:18:18:89:539:325:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:To our knowledge the Bank of Italy Survey of Household Income and Wealth (SHIW)
62:2090:127127:18:18:71:539:307:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:data is the only other data set that exists with the necessary variables to apply our model
62:2091:127218:18:18:71:539:289:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:estimates.64The SHIW collects detailed information on demographics, households consump-
62:2092:127305:18:17:72:539:271:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:tion and assets.65Following Bertola, Guiso, and Pistaferri [2005], we only use the waves after
62:2093:127399:17:18:71:539:254:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:1987 as the survey methodology has remained roughly constant over this time period. In
62:2094:127485:18:18:71:539:236:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:particular, we use the 1989, 1991, 1993, 1995,1998, 2000, 2002, 2004, 2006, 2008, 2010 and
62:2095:127575:18:18:71:539:218:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:2012 waves in our analysis. Each wave surveys a representative sample of 8000 Italian house-
62:2096:127667:18:18:71:539:200:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:holds. We focus our analysis on head of households. The value of non-durable consumption
62:2097:127755:18:18:71:540:182:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:inthedataisdefined as the sum of expenditure on apparel, schooling, entertainment, food,
62:2098:127843:18:18:71:539:164:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:medical expenses, housing repairs and additions and imputed rents. Our preferred measure
62:2099:127931:18:18:71:539:146:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:of the durable stock is the sum of end-of-period value of means of transport (includes autos,
62:2100:128024:18:17:71:539:128:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:motorcycles, caravans, boats and bicycles) and the value of real estate (housing and land).
62:2101:128115:17:16:81:539:111:49:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:F:64An alternative out-of-sample test would be to estimate the model on half of the PSID data and test it
62:2102:128218:16:8:72:362:95:100:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:F:on the other half of the PSID data.  This produces similar results.
62:2103:128285:8:16:81:428:87:49:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:A:65The dataset can be downloaded here: https://www.bancaditalia.it/statistiche
62:2104:128362:16:0:81:401:71:100:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:A:/indcamp/bilfait;internal&action=_setlanguage.action?LANGUAGE=en
63:2105:128428:0:18:72:540:708:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:Our results are robust to including other measures of durable adjustment including the value
63:2106:128520:18:18:72:540:690:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:of end-of-period stocks for furniture and jewelry. The SHIW also includes information on
63:2107:128608:18:18:72:540:672:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:durableflows for means of transport, furniture, and jewelry. Net assets are the sum of all
63:2108:128698:18:18:71:539:654:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:deposits, CDs, securities, businesses and valuables minus the value of all liabilities to banks,
63:2109:128794:18:27:71:250:636:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:corporations and other households.
63:2110:128828:27:239:173:438:609:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:C:Figure 26: Imputed Gap and Hazard in SHIW data
63:2111:128874:239:0:179:448:370:197:Helvetica:197:Helvetica:A:-1.2-0.600.61.2
63:2112:128889:0:0:183:191:379:197:Helvetica:197:Helvetica:?:0
63:2113:128890:0:0:163:190:419:197:Helvetica:197:Helvetica:?:0.05
63:2114:128894:0:0:171:190:457:197:Helvetica:197:Helvetica:?:0.1
63:2115:128897:0:0:163:190:496:197:Helvetica:197:Helvetica:?:0.15
63:2116:128901:0:0:171:190:534:197:Helvetica:197:Helvetica:?:0.2

63:2118:128915:218:0:275:355:355:197:Helvetica:197:Helvetica:A:Durable Gap
63:2119:128926:0:0:440:448:379:197:Helvetica:197:Helvetica:?:0
63:2120:128927:0:0:440:467:428:197:Helvetica:197:Helvetica:?:0.25
63:2121:128931:0:0:440:459:477:197:Helvetica:197:Helvetica:?:0.5
63:2122:128934:0:0:440:467:525:197:Helvetica:197:Helvetica:?:0.75
63:2123:128938:0:258:440:448:574:197:Helvetica:197:Helvetica:?:1
63:2124:128939:258:18:89:539:316:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:Next, we impose the same structural relationship from on the model on this data (as
63:2125:129022:18:18:71:539:298:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:we did in the PSID) to generate empirical measures of the empirical durable gap. Given
63:2126:129108:18:18:71:539:280:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:estimates of these gap, we can then calculate the probability of adjustment as a function
63:2127:129197:18:18:71:539:262:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:of the durable gap.  Unfortunately, unlike the PSID, there are fewer variables explicitly
63:2128:129286:18:18:71:539:244:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:asking households about their durable adjustment, so we must define adjustment purely in
63:2129:129374:18:18:71:539:226:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:terms of some adjustment threshold.  We define durable adjustment as times when the
63:2130:129457:18:18:71:539:208:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:household either had non-zero expenditure in a period on means of transport or a 40%
63:2131:129541:18:18:71:539:190:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:change in the reported value of real estate. The results are qualitatively robust (the hazard
63:2132:129634:18:18:71:539:172:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:rate is increasing in the durable gap) to using different minimum thresholds including the
63:2133:129724:18:18:71:540:154:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:25% threshold we used in our benchmark specification in the PSID. The main difference is
63:2134:129812:18:18:71:540:136:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:that a 25% threshold implies that the annual frequency of adjustment is approximately 30%,
63:2135:129902:18:18:71:495:118:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:whereas a 40% threshold implies an annual frequency of adjustment closer to 15%.
63:2136:129982:18:17:89:539:100:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:Figure 26 shows the distribution of gaps and hazards that arise in SHIW data when
63:2137:130063:17:0:71:540:83:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:applying our model which is estimated on PSID data.   Clearly, we continue tofind an
64:2138:130149:0:18:72:540:708:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:extremely strong upward sloping hazard.  In addition, the gap distribution continues to
64:2139:130236:18:18:72:193:690:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:have negative skewness.
64:2140:130259:18:18:89:539:672:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:In addition to computing the average distribution and hazard, we can also redo the
64:2141:130341:18:18:72:540:654:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:exercise in Section 4.2 using SHIW data.  Figure 27 is the counterpart to Figure 15 in
64:2142:130427:18:18:72:540:636:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:the SHIW. Since there is no comparable business cycle dating committee for Italy, we plot
64:2143:130516:18:18:72:544:618:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:theimpliedimpulseresponseonimpactfromSHIWagainsttheItalianunemploymentrate.
64:2144:130591:18:18:72:540:600:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:Just as in PSID data, the implied IRF is strongly procyclical.  As unemployment falls, the
64:2145:130681:18:26:72:213:582:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:IRF increases substantially.

64:2160:130866:267:30:72:375:211:301:g_d0_f1:301:g_d0_f1:C:11  Appendix 4: Model Extensions
64:2161:130898:30:18:72:540:181:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:In this section we extend our model to include two features which are particularly important
64:2162:130990:18:38:72:408:163:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:for housing markets: rental markets and collateralized borrowing.
64:2163:131055:38:26:72:226:125:209:g_d0_f1:209:g_d0_f1:C:11.1  Rental Markets
64:2164:131075:26:18:72:540:99:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:In this section we show that the inclusion of rental markets does not change any of the
64:2165:131162:18:0:72:540:81:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:conclusions from our model.  We choose not to include rental markets in our benchmark
65:2166:131249:0:18:72:540:708:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:model for several reasons: 1) Since we want to use a broad measure of durables as our
65:2167:131334:18:18:72:540:690:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:benchmark that encompasses all durable goods subject to lumpy adjustment costs, we must
65:2168:131421:18:18:72:539:672:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:necessarily aggregate differentdurablegoodsthataresomewhatdifferent into one good.
65:2169:131503:18:18:71:539:654:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:Overall, consumer durable spending is a larger fraction of total household durable spending
65:2170:131594:18:18:71:540:636:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:than is residential investment, so it makes sense for our benchmark calibration to reflect
65:2171:131684:18:18:71:539:618:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:something closer to consumer durable markets than housing markets. While rental markets
65:2172:131771:18:18:71:540:600:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:are clearly important for housing, rental markets are not particularly important for consumer
65:2173:131864:18:18:71:539:582:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:durables such as automobiles and furniture.  2) Our estimation procedure is based on the
65:2174:131952:18:18:71:539:564:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:concept of the "gap" between a household’s current durable holdings and those it would
65:2175:132038:18:18:71:539:546:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:choose if it temporarily faced no adjustment costs.   While this gap is well-defined for
65:2176:132126:18:18:71:539:528:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:durable owners, it is not well-defined for renters.  In general, even if temporarily facing no
65:2177:132220:18:18:71:539:510:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:adjustment costs, households may still choose not to purchase. 3) Finally, even for renters,
65:2178:132312:18:18:71:539:492:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:in reality moving is not costless. In order to remain computationally feasible, our extension
65:2179:132405:18:17:71:539:474:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:with rental markets assumes that households can costlessly adjust their durable holdings
65:2180:132493:17:18:71:539:457:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:when renting.  Thus, this model likely underestimates true frictions to durable adjustment.
65:2181:132584:18:18:71:540:439:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:Nevertheless, we now show that including this extension has only small effects on our results.
65:2182:132678:18:18:89:539:421:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:We extend the benchmark model by allowing households to rent durables. These durables
65:2183:132763:18:18:71:539:403:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:depreciate fully each period and are more expensive due to the presence of higher deprecia-
65:2184:132854:18:51:71:532:385:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:tion, but they are not subject to adjustment costs.  The household value function is then:
65:2185:132944:51:0:88:221:334:145:g_d0_f5:145:g_d0_f6:A:(−1−1)=max
65:2186:132960:0:10:213:218:344:145:g_d0_f9:145:g_d0_f9:C:£

65:2188:133017:0:31:523:528:344:145:g_d0_f9:145:g_d0_f9:C:¤

65:2199:133168:33:18:89:539:173:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:whereis the rental rate on durables and the 0 in the continuation value reflects the
65:2200:133254:18:18:71:539:155:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:fact that households who choose to rent today will start the following period with no durable
65:2201:133347:18:18:71:539:137:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:holdings.  The expressions for(−1−1)and(−1−1)are unchanged.
65:2202:133432:18:17:71:539:119:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:In addition to estimating the other parameters of the model, we pick the value ofto target
65:2203:133524:17:18:72:540:102:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:an owner occupancy rate of 0.65.  We show the estimated distribution and hazard for the
65:2204:133611:18:0:72:540:84:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:model with rental markets in Figures 28 and 30. Just as in our baseline model, we are able
66:2205:133703:0:18:72:248:708:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:to well-explain the empirical data.
66:2206:133738:18:18:89:540:690:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:After estimating the rental market model, we explore its aggregate implications.  Given
66:2207:133825:18:18:72:540:672:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:gaps and hazards in the PSID data from 1999-2011, we can use (1) to compute an implied
66:2208:133911:18:18:72:511:654:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:responsiveness across time.  Again wefind large variation that is strongly procyclical.
66:2209:133998:18:18:89:539:636:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:In addition, we can calculate the true impulse responses to shocks in our structural model.
66:2210:134089:18:18:72:540:618:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:Just as in the benchmark model, the model with rental markets delivers a strong state-
66:2211:134175:18:18:72:540:600:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:dependent IRF. Figure 32 shows the full impulse response to an aggregate income shock in
66:2212:134263:18:18:72:540:582:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:a boom period (1999) is much stronger than to the same income shock in a recession (2009).
66:2213:134353:18:18:72:540:564:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:For brevity, we do not show results for other aggregate shocks or for alternative measures of
66:2214:134446:18:39:72:313:546:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:responsiveness, but they deliver similar results.
66:2215:134495:39:25:72:293:507:209:g_d0_f1:209:g_d0_f1:C:11.2  Collateralized Borrowing
66:2216:134525:25:18:72:540:482:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:In this subsection, we explore the implications of a second extension which is important for
66:2217:134617:18:18:72:540:464:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:understanding some durable markets: the role of collateralized borrowing. In our benchmark
66:2218:134707:18:18:72:539:446:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:model, we assume that=1so that households cannot borrow against their durables.
66:2219:134787:18:18:71:539:428:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:In this section, we relax that assumption.  Since collateralized borrowing is particularly
66:2220:134877:18:18:71:539:410:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:important for housing, we think of this robustness check as one that applies more to housing
66:2221:134969:18:18:71:281:392:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:markets than to broad durable spending.
66:2222:135008:18:18:89:539:374:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:In this extension, we assume that households can borrow up to 80% of the value of their
66:2223:135095:18:18:71:539:356:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:durables: that is we assume that=02so that households must put down a 20% down-
66:2224:135176:18:18:71:539:338:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:payment.  The reason that we do not include collateralized borrowing in our benchmark
66:2225:135261:18:18:71:539:320:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:specification is because technical considerations force us to admit this borrowing in a way
66:2226:135352:18:18:71:539:302:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:that we view as somewhat empirically unrealistic.  Since it is not feasible to have a sep-
66:2227:135442:18:18:71:539:284:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:arate adjustment cost on durable equity together with afixed cost of durable adjustment
66:2228:135529:18:18:72:540:266:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:we must assume that durable equity can be adjusted costlessly.  That is, the collateralized
66:2229:135620:18:17:72:539:248:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:borrowing model which is numerically feasible must allow for costless refinancing.  Clearly
66:2230:135711:17:18:72:540:231:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:households in the real world cannot costlessly extract equity from their durables and do not
66:2231:135803:18:18:72:539:213:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:refinance continuously.  Thus, the specification with collateralized borrowing with friction-
66:2232:135896:18:18:72:540:195:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:less equity adjustment substantially overstates households’ ability to smooth idiosyncratic
66:2233:135987:18:18:72:475:177:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:earnings shocks and substantially understates the illiquidity of durable wealth.
66:2234:136067:18:18:89:539:159:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:Nevertheless, our basic message goes through: even with costless equity adjustment,
66:2235:136150:18:18:72:539:141:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:durable responsiveness remains procyclical. To solve the model with=02we reformulate
66:2236:136236:18:18:71:539:123:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:the problem using "voluntary equity" as in in Diaz and Luengo-Prado [2010].  This rec-
66:2237:136322:18:18:71:539:105:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:tangularizes the constraint set of households and simplifies the solution of the model.  The
66:2238:136414:18:0:71:539:87:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:reformulated model is solved identically to the model in our baseline results. Figure 33 and
67:2239:136508:0:312:154:458:699:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:C:Figure 28: Gap Distribution in Model with Rental Markets
67:2240:136564:312:290:127:484:387:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:C:Figure 29: Predicted and Actual Hazard: Model with Rental Markets
67:2241:136629:290:0:281:330:97:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:C:Figure 30
68:2242:136640:0:326:164:447:690:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:C:Figure 31: IRF on Impact: Model with Rental Markets
68:2243:136691:326:0:143:469:364:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:C:Figure 32: IRF in Boom and Bust: Model with Rental Markets
69:2244:136751:0:18:72:472:708:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:34 show the estimated gaps and hazards are a goodfit in the model and data.
69:2245:136826:18:18:89:539:690:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:Figures 35 and 36 show that we again get an impulse response function that is strongly
69:2246:136912:18:18:72:540:672:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:procyclical: during booms aggregate durable spending is much more responsive to income
69:2247:136998:18:57:72:226:654:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:shocks than during recessions.

74:2445:141380:27:18:89:539:404:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:It is important to note that while we have interpreted conditional heteroscedasticity as a
74:2446:141470:18:18:71:539:386:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:time-varying impulse response to aggregate shocks with a constant variance, an alternative
74:2447:141560:18:18:71:539:368:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:interpretation is that aggregate shocks themselves are larger during booms than during reces-
74:2448:141653:18:18:71:539:350:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:sions. We test for this by performing the same regressions on Baxter-King bandpassfiltered
74:2449:141743:18:18:71:540:332:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:GDP.69Table 6 shows that, as in the estimates in Bachmann, Caballero, and Engel [2013],
74:2450:141830:18:18:72:540:314:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:there is no evidence of conditional heteroscedasticity for GDP.  Presumably the aggregate
74:2451:141919:18:18:72:539:296:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:shocks hittingshould be similar to the aggregate shocks hitting, so we interpret our
74:2452:142005:18:18:72:544:278:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:resultsasevidencethatitisnottheshocksthatdriveheteroscedasticity,andisratherthe
74:2453:142084:18:17:72:540:260:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:mechanism that translates those shocks into durable expenditures that drives our estimates.
74:2454:142175:17:18:72:539:243:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:As further evidence for this point, we also estimate the time-series model for changes in
74:2455:142264:18:18:72:289:225:145:g_d0_f2:65:g_d0_f2:B:TFP70as well as the Federal Funds rate.71
74:2456:142305:18:18:89:540:207:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:To construct Figure 17, we allowed for a moreflexible non-parametric second stage.
74:2457:142387:18:18:71:540:189:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:We also bootstrapped the procedure to construct corresponding confidence intervals.  To
74:2458:142474:18:18:71:539:171:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:do this, we redraw residuals from thefirst stage to create one-thousand bootstrap samples
74:2459:142563:18:18:71:539:153:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:when estimating the non-parametric second-stage. We tried a variety of bandwidths for the
74:2460:142652:18:16:81:539:135:49:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:F:69Unlike expenditure rates, GDP is non-stationary and so must befiltered.  Using alternativefilters did
74:2461:142755:16:8:71:230:119:100:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:F:not substantively change the results.
74:2462:142792:8:12:81:456:111:49:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:A:70Available at http://www.frbsf.org/economics/economists/jfernald/quarterly_tfp.xls
74:2463:142875:12:15:81:539:99:49:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:F:71To increase the sample size we use monthly FF rates. While we could also use FF residuals or surprises,
74:2464:142980:15:12:72:540:84:100:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:F:it is likely that the actual rate is more relevant for durable purchases as households should respond to both
74:2465:143089:12:0:72:273:72:100:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:F:the anticipated and unanticipated component.
75:2466:143135:0:18:72:483:708:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:second-stage kernel estimator, and it did not change the qualitative conclusions.
75:2467:143216:18:18:89:540:690:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:In addition to these empirical estimates, we also compute heteroscedasticity estimates
75:2468:143302:18:18:72:540:672:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:for our simulated models. Since we know that the true shocks in the model follow an AR(1)
75:2469:143391:18:18:72:539:654:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:process, we report benchmark results restricted=1=1, but results are not sensitive to
75:2470:143479:18:18:71:539:636:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:this restriction.  Table A4 shows that the frictionless model does not generate procyclical
75:2471:143570:18:18:71:540:618:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:IRFs. If anything, the model withoutfixed costs implies0In contrast, the model with
75:2472:143656:18:18:72:539:600:145:g_d0_f3:145:g_d0_f2:B:fixed costs exhibits conditional heteroscedasticity that is in line with the empirical estimates.
75:2473:143753:18:18:72:539:582:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:The estimated0, and the time-variation in the impulse response on impact is similar to
75:2474:143841:18:18:72:157:564:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:that in the data.
75:2475:143858:18:18:89:540:546:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:A:Whilefixed costs induce procyclical impulse response functions, they need not be the
75:2476:143942:18:18:72:540:528:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:only mechanism that can generate these dynamics.   Another possible explanation for a
75:2477:144027:18:18:72:540:510:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:procyclical IRF is the presence of collateral constraints.  If collateral constraints tighten
75:2478:144120:18:18:72:540:492:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:during recessions, then durable expenditures may respond less to shocks during recessions
75:2479:144209:18:17:72:540:474:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:than during booms. We have tested for this using the models with collateral constraints in
75:2480:144299:17:18:72:540:457:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:Appendix 4. However, wefind that this mechanism is quantitatively weak. This is partially
75:2481:144388:18:18:71:539:439:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:because the response to time-varying credit conditions is solely one-sided.  A tightening of
75:2482:144480:18:18:71:540:421:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:credit conditions has a direct effect on households that want to increase durables but has no
75:2483:144573:18:18:71:539:403:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:direct effect on households that want to sell durables. This substantially dampens the scope
75:2484:144665:18:18:71:539:385:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:for time-varying IRFs.  Furthermore, wefind no empirical support for such asymmetric
75:2485:144749:18:0:71:164:367:145:g_d0_f2:145:g_d0_f2:B:heteroscedasticity.
